<p><b>Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi</b> (<a href="/wiki/Gujarati_language" title="Gujarati language">Gujarati</a>: <span lang="gu" xml:lang="gu">મોહનદાસ કરમચંદ ગાંધી</span>; <a href="/wiki/Hindi_language" title="Hindi language" class="mw-redirect">Hindi</a>: <span lang="hi" xml:lang="hi">मोहनदास करमचंद गांधी</span>, pronounced <span title="Pronunciation in IPA" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:IPA_for_Hindi_and_Urdu" title="Wikipedia:IPA for Hindi and Urdu">[moːɦənd̪aːs kərəmtɕənd̪ ɡaːnd̪ʱi]</a></span><small>&#160; <span style="white-space:nowrap;">(<img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" width="13" height="13" /> <a href="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3e/Hi-Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi_pronunciation.ogg" class="internal" title="Hi-Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi pronunciation.ogg">listen</a>)</span></small>; 2 October 1869&#160;– 30 January 1948) was a pre-eminent political and ideological leader of <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">India</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement">Indian independence movement</a>. He pioneered <i><a href="/wiki/Satyagraha">satyagraha</a></i>. This is defined as resistance to <a href="/wiki/Tyranny" class="mw-redirect" title="Tyranny">tyranny</a> through mass <a href="/wiki/Civil_resistance">civil resistance</a> - a term which Gandhi used in many of his statements and writings.<sup id="cite_ref-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-0"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> His philosophy was firmly founded upon <i><a href="/wiki/Ahimsa">ahimsa</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Nonviolence">nonviolence</a>). His philosophy and leadership helped India gain independence and inspired movements for civil rights and freedom across the world. Gandhi is often referred to as <b>Mahatma</b> (<span title="Pronunciation in IPA" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:IPA_for_Hindi_and_Urdu" title="Wikipedia:IPA for Hindi and Urdu">[məɦaːt̪maː]</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: महात्मा <i><a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81tm%C4%81">mahātmā</a></i> or "Great Soul" (magnanimous), an <a href="/wiki/Honorific">honorific</a> first applied to him by <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> In India he is also called <b>Bapu</b> (<a href="/wiki/Gujarati_language" title="Gujarati language">Gujarati</a>: <span lang="gu" xml:lang="gu">બાપુ</span>, <i>bāpu</i> or "Father") and officially honored in India as the <i><a href="/wiki/Father_of_the_Nation">Father of the Nation</a></i>. His birthday, 2 October, is commemorated as <i><a href="/wiki/Gandhi_Jayanti">Gandhi Jayanti</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Holidays_in_India" title="Holidays in India" class="mw-redirect">national holiday</a>, and worldwide as the <a href="/wiki/International_Day_of_Non-Violence">International Day of Non-Violence</a>. Gandhi was assassinated on 30 January 1948 by <a href="/wiki/Nathuram_Godse">Nathuram Godse</a>.</p>
<p>Gandhi first employed <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience">civil disobedience</a> while an expatriate lawyer in South Africa, during the resident Indian community's struggle for civil rights. After his return to India in 1915, he organized protests by peasants, farmers, and urban laborers concerning excessive land-tax and discrimination. After assuming leadership of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_National_Congress">Indian National Congress</a> in 1921, Gandhi led nationwide campaigns to ease poverty, expand women's rights, build religious and ethnic amity, end <a href="/wiki/Dalit" title="Dalit">untouchability</a>, and increase economic self-reliance. Above all, he aimed to achieve <i><a href="/wiki/Swaraj">Swaraj</a></i> or the independence of India from foreign domination. Gandhi famously led his followers in the <a href="/wiki/Non-cooperation_movement">Non-cooperation movement</a> that protested the British-imposed salt tax with the 400&#160;km (240&#160;mi) <a href="/wiki/Salt_Satyagraha" title="Salt Satyagraha">Dandi Salt March</a> in 1930. He launched the <a href="/wiki/Quit_India_Movement">Quit India Movement</a> in 1942, demanding immediate independence for India. Gandhi spent a number of years in jail in both South Africa and India.</p>
<p>As a practitioner of <i><a href="/wiki/Ahimsa">ahimsa</a></i>, Gandhi swore to speak the truth and advocated that others do the same. He lived modestly in a <a href="/wiki/Sabarmati_Ashram" title="Sabarmati Ashram">self-sufficient residential community</a> and wore the traditional Indian <i><a href="/wiki/Dhoti">dhoti</a></i> and shawl, woven from yarn that he had spun by hand himself. He ate simple vegetarian food, experimented for a time with a <a href="/wiki/Fruitarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Fruitarian">fruitarian</a> diet, and undertook long <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasts</a> as a means of both self-purification and social protest.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_background">Early life and background</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_Gandhi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Young_Gandhi.jpg/170px-Young_Gandhi.jpg" width="170" height="320" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_Gandhi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A young Gandhi, <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">c.</a> 1876</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> was born on 2 October 1869 in <a href="/wiki/Porbandar">Porbandar</a>, a coastal town which was then part of the <a href="/wiki/Bombay_Presidency">Bombay Presidency</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a>. His father, Karamchand Gandhi (1822–1885), who belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Hindu">Hindu</a> <a href="/wiki/Modh">Modh</a> community, served as the <i><a href="/wiki/Diwan_(title)" title="Diwan (title)" class="mw-redirect">diwan</a></i> (a high official) of <a href="/wiki/Porbandar" title="Porbandar">Porbander state</a>, a small <a href="/wiki/Princely_state">princely state</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kathiawar_Agency">Kathiawar Agency</a> of <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fischer1954_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fischer1954-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> His grandfather was Uttamchand Gandhi, fondly called Utta Gandhi. His mother, Putlibai, who came from the Hindu Pranami <a href="/wiki/Vaishnava" class="mw-redirect" title="Vaishnava">Vaishnava</a> community, was Karamchand's fourth wife, the first three wives having apparently died in childbirth.<sup id="cite_ref-tendulkar_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tendulkar-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Growing up with a devout mother and the <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jain</a> traditions of the region, the young Mohandas absorbed early the influences that would play an important role in his adult life; these included compassion for sentient beings, vegetarianism, <a href="/wiki/Fasting">fasting</a> for self-purification, and mutual tolerance among individuals of different creeds.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Indian classics, especially the stories of <a href="/wiki/Shravan#In_Hindu_epics" title="Shravan">Shravana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harishchandra" title="Harishchandra">Maharaja Harishchandra</a>, had a great impact on Gandhi in his childhood. Gandhi, in his autobiography, admits that it left an indelible impression on his mind. He writes: "It haunted me and I must have acted Harishchandra to myself times without number." Gandhi's early self-identification with Truth and Love as supreme values is traceable to these epic characters.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1883, the 13-year old Mohandas was married to 14-year old <a href="/wiki/Kasturba_Gandhi" title="Kasturba Gandhi">Kasturbai Makhanji</a> (her first name was usually shortened to "Kasturba", and affectionately to "Ba") in an <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged child marriage</a>, according to the custom of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-autobio-wedding_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autobio-wedding-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Recalling the day of their marriage he once said, "As we didn't know much about marriage, for us it meant only wearing new clothes, eating sweets and playing with relatives." However, as was also the custom of the region, the adolescent bride was to spend much time at her parents' house, and away from her husband.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1885, when Gandhi was 15, the couple's first child was born, but survived only a few days, and Gandhi's father, Karamchand Gandhi, had died earlier that year.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Mohandas and Kasturba had four more children, all sons: <a href="/wiki/Harilal_Gandhi" title="Harilal Gandhi">Harilal</a>, born in 1888; <a href="/wiki/Manilal_Gandhi" title="Manilal Gandhi">Manilal</a>, born in 1892; <a href="/wiki/Ramdas_Gandhi" title="Ramdas Gandhi">Ramdas</a>, born in 1897; and <a href="/wiki/Devdas_Gandhi" title="Devdas Gandhi">Devdas</a>, born in 1900. At his middle school in Porbandar and high school in Rajkot, Gandhi remained an average student. He passed the <a href="/wiki/Matriculation" title="Matriculation">matriculation exam</a> for Samaldas College at <a href="/wiki/Bhavnagar">Bhavnagar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gujarat">Gujarat</a> with some difficulty. While there, he was unhappy, in part because his family wanted him to become a <a href="/wiki/Barrister">barrister</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_and_Kasturbhai_1902.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Gandhi_and_Kasturbhai_1902.jpg/220px-Gandhi_and_Kasturbhai_1902.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_and_Kasturbhai_1902.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Gandhi and his wife <a href="/wiki/Kasturba_Gandhi" title="Kasturba Gandhi">Kasturba</a> (1902)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 4 September 1888, Gandhi travelled to London, England, to study law at <a href="/wiki/University_College_London">University College London</a> and to train as a <a href="/wiki/Barrister">barrister</a>. His time in London, the Imperial capital, was influenced by a vow he had made to his mother in the presence of the Jain monk Becharji, upon leaving India, to observe the Hindu precepts of abstinence from meat, alcohol, and promiscuity.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Gandhi experimented with adopting "English" customs—taking dancing lessons for example—he could not stomach the bland vegetarian food offered by his landlady and he was always hungry until he found one of London's few vegetarian restaurants. Influenced by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stephens_Salt" title="Henry Stephens Salt">Salt's</a> book, he joined the <a href="/wiki/Vegetarian_Society">Vegetarian Society</a>, was elected to its executive committee,<sup id="cite_ref-brown_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> and started a local Bayswater chapter.<sup id="cite_ref-tendulkar_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tendulkar-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Some of the vegetarians he met were members of the <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society">Theosophical Society</a>, which had been founded in 1875 to further universal brotherhood, and which was devoted to the study of <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindu">Hindu</a> literature. They encouraged Gandhi to join them in reading the <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavad_Gita">Bhagavad Gita</a></i> both in translation as well as in the original.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Not having shown a particular interest in religion before, he became interested in religious thought and began to read both <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> as well as Christian scriptures.<sup id="cite_ref-tendulkar_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tendulkar-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-brown_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi was called to the bar on 10 June 1891 and left London for India on 12 June 1891,<sup id="cite_ref-tendulkar_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tendulkar-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> where he learned that his mother had died while he was in London, his family having kept the news from him.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> His attempts at establishing a law practice in <a href="/wiki/Bombay" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombay">Bombay</a> failed and, later, after applying and being turned down for a part-time job as a high school teacher, he ended up returning to <a href="/wiki/Rajkot">Rajkot</a> to make a modest living drafting petitions for litigants, a business he was forced to close when he ran foul of a British officer. In his autobiography he refers to this incident as an unsuccessful attempt to lobby on behalf of his older brother.<sup id="cite_ref-tendulkar_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tendulkar-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-brown_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> It was in this climate that, in April 1893, he accepted a year-long contract from Dada Abdulla &amp; Co., an Indian firm, to a post in the <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Natal">Colony of Natal</a>, South Africa, then part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire">British Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tendulkar_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tendulkar-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights_movement_in_South_Africa_.281893.E2.80.931914.29">Civil rights movement in South Africa (1893–1914)</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi_in_South_Africa">Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi in South Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_South-Africa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Gandhi_South-Africa.jpg/220px-Gandhi_South-Africa.jpg" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_South-Africa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Gandhi in South Africa (1895)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:176px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Boer_War_1899.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f8/Gandhi_Boer_War_1899.jpg" width="174" height="350" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Boer_War_1899.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
M.K. Gandhi while serving in the Ambulance Corps during the Boer War (1899)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In South Africa, Gandhi faced the discrimination directed at Indians. He was thrown off a train at <a href="/wiki/Pietermaritzburg">Pietermaritzburg</a> after refusing to move from the first class to a third-class coach while holding a valid first-class ticket.<sup id="cite_ref-essential_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-essential-12"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Travelling farther on by stagecoach he was beaten by a driver for refusing to travel on the foot board to make room for a European passenger.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> He suffered other hardships on the journey as well, including being barred from several hotels. In another incident, the magistrate of a <a href="/wiki/Durban">Durban</a> court ordered Gandhi to remove his <a href="/wiki/Turban">turban</a>, which he refused to do.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> These events were a turning point in his life, awakening him to social injustice and influencing his subsequent social activism. It was through witnessing firsthand the <a href="/wiki/Racism">racism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prejudice">prejudice</a> and injustice against Indians in South Africa that Gandhi started to question his people's status within the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire">British Empire</a>, and his own place in society.</p>
<p>Gandhi extended his original period of stay in South Africa to assist Indians in opposing a bill to deny them the right to vote. Though unable to halt the bill's passage, his campaign was successful in drawing attention to the grievances of Indians in South Africa. He helped found the <a href="/wiki/Natal_Indian_Congress">Natal Indian Congress</a> in 1894,<sup id="cite_ref-tendulkar_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tendulkar-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-essential_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-essential-12"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> and through this organisation, he moulded the Indian community of South Africa into a unified political force. In January 1897, when Gandhi landed in Durban he was attacked by a mob of white settlers and escaped only through the efforts of the wife of the police superintendent. He, however, refused to press charges against any member of the mob, stating it was one of his principles not to seek redress for a personal wrong in a court of law.<sup id="cite_ref-tendulkar_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tendulkar-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1906, the <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Colony" title="Transvaal Colony">Transvaal</a> government promulgated a new Act compelling registration of the colony's Indian population. At a mass protest meeting held in Johannesburg on 11 September that year, Gandhi adopted his still evolving methodology of <i><a href="/wiki/Satyagraha">satyagraha</a></i> (devotion to the truth), or non-violent protest, for the first time, calling on his fellow Indians to defy the new law and suffer the punishments for doing so, rather than resist through violent means. The community adopted this plan, leading to a seven-year struggle in which thousands of Indians were jailed (including Gandhi), flogged, or even shot, for striking, refusing to register, burning their registration cards or engaging in other forms of non-violent resistance. While the government was successful in repressing the Indian protesters, the public outcry stemming from the harsh methods employed by the South African government in the face of peaceful Indian protesters finally forced South African General <a href="/wiki/Jan_Christiaan_Smuts" class="mw-redirect" title="Jan Christiaan Smuts">Jan Christiaan Smuts</a> to negotiate a compromise with Gandhi. Gandhi's ideas took shape and the concept of <i>satyagraha</i> matured during this struggle.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racism_and_controversy">Racism and controversy</span></h3>
<p>Some of Gandhi's early South African articles are controversial. On 7 March 1908, Gandhi wrote in the <i><a href="/wiki/Indian_Opinion">Indian Opinion</a></i> of his time in a South African prison: "<a href="/wiki/Kaffir_(racial_term)" title="Kaffir (racial term)">Kaffirs</a> are as a rule uncivilised—the convicts even more so. They are troublesome, very dirty and live almost like animals."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Writing on the subject of immigration in 1903, Gandhi commented: "We believe as much in the purity of race as we think they do... We believe also that the white race in South Africa should be the predominating race."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> During his time in South Africa, Gandhi protested repeatedly about the social classification of blacks with Indians, whom he described as "undoubtedly infinitely superior to the Kaffirs".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> Remarks such as these have led some to accuse Gandhi of racism.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_racist_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_racist-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> It is worth noting though that the word Kaffir had a different connotation in Gandhi's time than its current day meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Two professors of history who specialise in South Africa, Surendra Bhana and Goolam Vahed, examined this controversy in their text, <i>The Making of a Political Reformer: Gandhi in South Africa, 1893–1914.</i> (New Delhi: Manohar, 2005). They focus in Chapter 1, "Gandhi, Africans and Indians in Colonial Natal" on the relationship between the African and Indian communities under "White rule" and policies which enforced segregation (and, they argue, led to inevitable conflict between these communities). Of this relationship they state that, "the young Gandhi was influenced by segregationist notions prevalent in the 1890s."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> At the same time, they state, "Gandhi's experiences in jail seemed to make him more sensitive to their plight...the later Gandhi mellowed; he seemed much less categorical in his expression of prejudice against Africans, and much more open to seeing points of common cause. His negative views in the Johannesburg jail were reserved for hardened African prisoners rather than Africans generally."<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> However, when plans to unveil a statue of Gandhi in <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a> were announced, a movement unsuccessfully tried to block it because of Gandhi's "racist" statements.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_racist_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_racist-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_Zulu_War_of_1906">Role in Zulu War of 1906</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bambatha_Rebellion">Bambatha Rebellion</a></div>
<p>In 1906, after the British introduced a new poll-tax, <a href="/wiki/Zulu_people" title="Zulu people">Zulus</a> in South Africa killed two British officers. In response, the British declared <a href="/wiki/Zulu_War_of_1906" title="Zulu War of 1906" class="mw-redirect">war against the Zulus</a>. Gandhi actively encouraged the British to recruit Indians. He argued that Indians should support the war efforts in order to legitimise their claims to full citizenship. The British, however, refused to commission Indians as army officers. Nonetheless, they accepted Gandhi's offer to let a detachment of Indians volunteer as a stretcher-bearer corps to treat wounded British soldiers. This corps was commanded by Gandhi. On 21 July 1906, Gandhi wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/Indian_Opinion">Indian Opinion</a></i>: "The corps had been formed at the instance of the Natal Government by way of experiment, in connection with the operations against the Natives consists of twenty three Indians".<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi urged the Indian population in South Africa to join the war through his columns in <i>Indian Opinion</i>: “If the Government only realised what reserve force is being wasted, they would make use of it and give Indians the opportunity of a thorough training for actual warfare.”<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Gandhi's opinion, the Draft Ordinance of 1906 brought the status of Indians below the level of Natives. He therefore urged Indians to resist the Ordinance along the lines of <i><a href="/wiki/Satyagraha">satyagraha</a></i> by taking the example of "<a href="/wiki/Kaffir_(ethnic_slur)" title="Kaffir (ethnic slur)" class="mw-redirect">Kaffirs</a>". In his words, "Even the half-castes and kaffirs, who are less advanced than we, have resisted the government. The pass law applies to them as well, but they do not take out passes."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1927 Gandhi wrote of the event: "The <a href="/wiki/Boer_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer War">Boer War</a> had not brought home to me the horrors of war with anything like the vividness that the [Zulu] 'rebellion' did. This was no war but a man-hunt, not only in my opinion, but also in that of many Englishmen with whom I had occasion to talk."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Struggle_for_Indian_Independence_.281915.E2.80.931945.29">Struggle for Indian Independence (1915–1945)</span></h2>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement">Indian independence movement</a></div>
<p>In 1915, Gandhi returned from South Africa to live in India. He spoke at the conventions of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_National_Congress">Indian National Congress</a>, but was primarily introduced to Indian issues, politics and the Indian people by <a href="/wiki/Gopal_Krishna_Gokhale">Gopal Krishna Gokhale</a>, a respected leader of the Congress Party at the time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_World_War_I">Role in World War I</span></h3>
<p>In April 1918, during the latter part of World War I, the <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Thesiger,_1st_Viscount_Chelmsford" title="Frederic Thesiger, 1st Viscount Chelmsford">Viceroy</a> invited Gandhi to a War Conference in Delhi<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Perhaps to show his support for the Empire and help his case for India's independence,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi agreed to actively recruit Indians for the war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-wikilivres.info_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wikilivres.info-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> In contrast to the Zulu War of 1906 and the outbreak of World War I in 1914, when he recruited volunteers for the Ambulance Corps, this time Gandhi attempted to recruit combatants. In a June 1918 leaflet entitled "Appeal for Enlistment", Gandhi wrote "To bring about such a state of things we should have the ability to defend ourselves, that is, the ability to bear arms and to use them...If we want to learn the use of arms with the greatest possible despatch, it is our duty to enlist ourselves in the army."<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> He did however stipulate in a letter to the <a href="/wiki/John_Maffey,_1st_Baron_Rugby" title="John Maffey, 1st Baron Rugby">Viceroy's private secretary</a> that he "personally will not kill or injure anybody, friend or foe."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi's war recruitment campaign brought into question his consistency on nonviolence as his friend <a href="/wiki/Charles_Freer_Andrews" title="Charles Freer Andrews">Charlie Andrews</a> confirms, "Personally I have never been able to reconcile this with his own conduct in other respects, and it is one of the points where I have found myself in painful disagreement."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mahadev_Desai" title="Mahadev Desai">Gandhi's private secretary</a> also acknowledges that "The question of the consistency between his creed of 'Ahimsa' (non-violence) and his recruiting campaign was raised not only then but has been discussed ever since."<sup id="cite_ref-wikilivres.info_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wikilivres.info-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Champaran_and_Kheda">Champaran and Kheda</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Champaran_and_Kheda_Satyagraha">Champaran and Kheda Satyagraha</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Kheda_1918.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gandhi_Kheda_1918.jpg/170px-Gandhi_Kheda_1918.jpg" width="170" height="268" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Kheda_1918.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Gandhi in 1918, at the time of the Kheda and Champaran satyagrahas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gandhi's first major achievements came in 1918 with the <a href="/wiki/Champaran">Champaran</a> agitation and <i>Kheda Satyagraha</i>, although in the latter it was <a href="/wiki/Indigo_plant" title="Indigo plant" class="mw-redirect">indigo</a> and other cash crops instead of the food crops necessary for their survival. Suppressed by the militias of the landlords (mostly British), they were given measly compensation, leaving them mired in extreme poverty. The villages were kept extremely dirty and unhygienic; and alcoholism was rampant. Now in the throes of a devastating famine, the British levied a tax which they insisted on increasing. The situation was desperate. In <a href="/wiki/Kheda">Kheda</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, the problem was the same. Gandhi established an <a href="/wiki/Ashram">ashram</a> there, organising scores of his veteran supporters and fresh volunteers from the region. He organised a detailed study and survey of the villages, accounting for the atrocities and terrible episodes of suffering, including the general state of degenerate living. Building on the confidence of villagers, he began leading the cleanup of villages, building of schools and hospitals and encouraging the village leadership to undo and condemn many social evils such as untouchability and alcoholism.</p>
<p>But his main impact came when he was arrested by police on the charge of creating unrest and was ordered to leave the province. Hundreds of thousands of people protested and rallied outside the jail, police stations and courts demanding his release, which the court reluctantly granted. Gandhi led organised protests and strikes against the landlords who, with the guidance of the British government, signed an agreement granting the poor farmers of the region more compensation and control over farming, and cancellation of revenue hikes and its collection until the famine ended. It was during this agitation that Gandhi was addressed by the people as <i>Bapu</i> (Father) and <i>Mahatma</i> (Great Soul). In Kheda, <a href="/wiki/Sardar_Patel" class="mw-redirect" title="Sardar Patel">Sardar Patel</a> represented the farmers in negotiations with the British, who suspended revenue collection and released all the prisoners. As a result, Gandhi became well-known in India.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-cooperation">Non-cooperation</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-cooperation_movement">Non-cooperation movement</a></div>
<p>Gandhi employed non-cooperation, non-violence and peaceful resistance as his "weapons" in the struggle against the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj">British Raj</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Punjab_(British_India)" title="Punjab (British India)" class="mw-redirect">Punjab</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jallianwala_Bagh_massacre">Jallianwala Bagh massacre</a> of civilians by British troops (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Amritsar_Massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Amritsar Massacre">Amritsar Massacre</a>) caused deep trauma to the nation, leading to increased public anger and acts of violence. Gandhi criticised both the actions of the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj">British Raj</a> and the retaliatory violence of Indians. He authored the resolution offering condolences to British civilian victims and condemning the riots which, after initial opposition in the party, was accepted following Gandhi's emotional speech advocating his principle that all violence was evil and could not be justified.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> But it was after the massacre and subsequent violence that Gandhi's mind focused upon obtaining complete self-government and control of all Indian government institutions, maturing soon into <i><a href="/wiki/Swaraj">Swaraj</a></i> or complete individual, spiritual, political independence.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahatma_Gandhi%27s_room_at_Sabarmati_Ashram.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mahatma_Gandhi%27s_room_at_Sabarmati_Ashram.jpg/220px-Mahatma_Gandhi%27s_room_at_Sabarmati_Ashram.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahatma_Gandhi%27s_room_at_Sabarmati_Ashram.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Mahatma Gandhi's room at <a href="/wiki/Sabarmati_Ashram">Sabarmati Ashram</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_home.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Gandhi_home.jpg/220px-Gandhi_home.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_home.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sabarmati_Ashram">Sabarmati Ashram</a>, Gandhi's home in Gujarat</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In December 1921, Gandhi was invested with executive authority on behalf of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_National_Congress">Indian National Congress</a>. Under his leadership, the Congress was reorganised with a new constitution, with the goal of <i>Swaraj</i>. Membership in the party was opened to anyone prepared to pay a token fee. A hierarchy of committees was set up to improve discipline, transforming the party from an elite organisation to one of mass national appeal. Gandhi expanded his non-violence platform to include the <a href="/wiki/Swadeshi_movement" title="Swadeshi movement"><i>swadeshi</i> policy</a>&#160;— the boycott of foreign-made goods, especially British goods. Linked to this was his advocacy that <i><a href="/wiki/Khadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Khadi">khadi</a></i> (homespun cloth) be worn by all Indians instead of British-made textiles. Gandhi exhorted Indian men and women, rich or poor, to spend time each day spinning <i>khadi</i> in support of the independence movement.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi even invented a small portable spinning wheel that could be folded into the size of a small typewriter.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> This was a strategy to inculcate discipline and dedication to weed out the unwilling and ambitious, and to include women in the movement at a time when many thought that such activities were not respectable activities for women. In addition to boycotting British products, Gandhi urged the people to boycott British educational institutions and law courts, to resign from government employment, and to forsake <a href="/wiki/British_honours_system" title="British honours system" class="mw-redirect">British titles and honours</a>.</p>
<p>"Non-cooperation" enjoyed widespread appeal and success, increasing excitement and participation from all strata of Indian society. Yet, just as the movement reached its apex, it ended abruptly as a result of a violent clash in the town of <a href="/wiki/Chauri_Chaura">Chauri Chaura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, in February 1922. Fearing that the movement was about to take a turn towards violence, and convinced that this would be the undoing of all his work, Gandhi called off the campaign of mass civil disobedience.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Roberts">Andrew Roberts</a>, this was the third time that Gandhi had called off a major campaign, "leaving in the lurch more than 15,000 supporters who were jailed for the cause".<sup id="cite_ref-GreatSoulReview_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreatSoulReview-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi was arrested on 10 March 1922, tried for sedition, and sentenced to six years' imprisonment. He began his sentence on 18 March 1922. He was released in February 1924 for an <a href="/wiki/Appendicitis">appendicitis</a> operation, having served only 2 years.</p>
<p>Without Gandhi's unifying personality, the Indian National Congress began to splinter during his years in prison, splitting into two factions, one led by <a href="/wiki/Chitta_Ranjan_Das" class="mw-redirect" title="Chitta Ranjan Das">Chitta Ranjan Das</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motilal_Nehru">Motilal Nehru</a> favouring party participation in the legislatures, and the other led by <a href="/wiki/Chakravarti_Rajagopalachari" class="mw-redirect" title="Chakravarti Rajagopalachari">Chakravarti Rajagopalachari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sardar_Vallabhbhai_Patel" class="mw-redirect" title="Sardar Vallabhbhai Patel">Sardar Vallabhbhai Patel</a>, opposing this move. Furthermore, cooperation among Hindus and Muslims, which had been strong at the height of the non-violence campaign, was breaking down. Gandhi attempted to bridge these differences through many means, including a three-week fast in the autumn of 1924, but with limited success.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> This may have been due to Gandhi's "uncanny ability to irritate and frustrate" India's Muslim leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-GreatSoulReview_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreatSoulReview-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salt_Satyagraha_.28Salt_March.29">Salt Satyagraha (Salt March)</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Salt_Satyagraha">Salt Satyagraha</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_at_Dandi_5_April_1930.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Gandhi_at_Dandi_5_April_1930.jpg/170px-Gandhi_at_Dandi_5_April_1930.jpg" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_at_Dandi_5_April_1930.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Gandhi at Dandi, 5 April 1930, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Salt_Satyagraha" title="Salt Satyagraha">Salt March</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gandhi stayed out of active politics and, as such, the limelight for most of the 1920s. He focused instead on resolving the wedge between the Swaraj Party and the Indian National Congress, and expanding initiatives against untouchability, alcoholism, ignorance and poverty. He returned to the fore in 1928. In the preceding year, the British government had appointed a new constitutional reform commission under Sir John Simon, which did not include any Indian as its member. The result was a boycott of the commission by Indian political parties. Gandhi pushed through a resolution at the Calcutta Congress in December 1928 calling on the British government to grant India dominion status or face a new campaign of non-cooperation with complete independence for the country as its goal. Gandhi had not only moderated the views of younger men like <a href="/wiki/Subhas_Chandra_Bose">Subhas Chandra Bose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a>, who sought a demand for immediate independence, but also reduced his own call to a one year wait, instead of two.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> The British did not respond. On 31 December 1929, the flag of India was unfurled in <a href="/wiki/Lahore">Lahore</a>. 26 January 1930 was celebrated as India's Independence Day by the Indian National Congress meeting in Lahore. This day was commemorated by almost every other Indian organisation. Gandhi then launched a new satyagraha against the tax on salt in March 1930. This was highlighted by the famous Salt March to Dandi from 12 March to 6 April, where he marched 388 kilometres (241 miles) from Ahmedabad to Dandi, Gujarat to make salt himself. Thousands of Indians joined him on this march to the sea. This campaign was one of his most successful at upsetting British hold on India; Britain responded by imprisoning over 60,000&#160;people.</p>
<p>The government, represented by <a href="/wiki/E._F._L._Wood,_1st_Earl_of_Halifax" title="E. F. L. Wood, 1st Earl of Halifax">Lord Edward Irwin</a>, decided to negotiate with Gandhi. The <a href="/wiki/Gandhi%E2%80%93Irwin_Pact">Gandhi–Irwin Pact</a> was signed in March 1931. The British Government agreed to free all political prisoners, in return for the suspension of the civil disobedience movement. Also as a result of the pact, Gandhi was invited to attend the Round Table Conference in London as the sole representative of the Indian National Congress. The conference was a disappointment to Gandhi and the nationalists, because it focused on the Indian princes and Indian minorities rather than on a transfer of power. Furthermore, Lord Irwin's successor, <a href="/wiki/Freeman_Freeman-Thomas,_1st_Marquess_of_Willingdon" title="Freeman Freeman-Thomas, 1st Marquess of Willingdon">Lord Willingdon</a>, began a new campaign of controlling and subduing the nationalist movement. Gandhi was again arrested, and the government tried to negate his influence by completely isolating him from his followers. But this tactic failed.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahadev_Desai_and_Gandhi_2_1939.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Mahadev_Desai_and_Gandhi_2_1939.jpg/170px-Mahadev_Desai_and_Gandhi_2_1939.jpg" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahadev_Desai_and_Gandhi_2_1939.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mahadev_Desai">Mahadev Desai</a> (left) reading out a letter to Gandhi from the <a href="/wiki/Viceroy">viceroy</a> at Birla House, Bombay, 7 April 1939</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1932, through the campaigning of the Dalit leader <a href="/wiki/B._R._Ambedkar">B. R. Ambedkar</a>, the government granted untouchables separate electorates under the new constitution. In protest, Gandhi embarked on a six-day fast in September 1932. The resulting public outcry successfully forced the government to adopt a more equitable arrangement via negotiations mediated by the Dalit cricketer turned political leader <a href="/wiki/Palwankar_Baloo">Palwankar Baloo</a>. This was the start of a new campaign by Gandhi to improve the lives of the untouchables, whom he named Harijans, the children of God. On 8 May 1933, Gandhi began a 21-day fast of self-purification to help the Harijan movement.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> This new campaign was not universally embraced within the <a href="/wiki/Dalit_(outcaste)" title="Dalit (outcaste)" class="mw-redirect">Dalit</a> community, as prominent leader <a href="/wiki/B._R._Ambedkar">B. R. Ambedkar</a> condemned Gandhi's use of the term <i>Harijans</i> as saying that Dalits were socially immature, and that privileged caste Indians played a paternalistic role. Ambedkar and his allies also felt Gandhi was undermining Dalit political rights. Gandhi had also refused to support the untouchables in 1924-25 when they were campaigning for the right to pray in temples. Because of Gandhi's actions, Ambedkar described him as "devious and untrustworthy".<sup id="cite_ref-GreatSoulReview_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreatSoulReview-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi, although born into the <a href="/wiki/Vaishya">Vaishya</a> caste, insisted that he was able to speak on behalf of Dalits, despite the presence of Dalit activists such as Ambedkar.</p>
<p>In the summer of 1934, three unsuccessful attempts were made on Gandhi's life.</p>
<p>When the Congress Party chose to contest elections and accept power under the Federation scheme, Gandhi resigned from party membership. He did not disagree with the party's move, but felt that if he resigned, his popularity with Indians would cease to stifle the party's membership, which actually varied, including communists, socialists, trade unionists, students, religious conservatives, and those with pro-business convictions, and that these various voices would get a chance to make themselves heard. Gandhi also wanted to avoid being a target for Raj propaganda by leading a party that had temporarily accepted political accommodation with the Raj.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi returned to active politics again in 1936, with the Nehru presidency and the Lucknow session of the Congress. Although Gandhi wanted a total focus on the task of winning independence and not speculation about India's future, he did not restrain the Congress from adopting socialism as its goal. Gandhi had a clash with <a href="/wiki/Subhas_Bose" class="mw-redirect" title="Subhas Bose">Subhas Bose</a>, who had been elected president in 1938. Their main points of contention were Bose's lack of commitment to democracy<sup class="Template-Fact" title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from December 2010" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">citation needed</a></i>]</sup> and lack of faith in non-violence. Bose won his second term despite Gandhi's criticism, but left the Congress when the All-India leaders resigned en masse in protest of his abandonment of the principles introduced by Gandhi.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_and_Quit_India">World War II and <i>Quit India</i></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quit_India_Movement">Quit India Movement</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nehruwithgandhi1942.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Nehruwithgandhi1942.jpg/220px-Nehruwithgandhi1942.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nehruwithgandhi1942.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a> sitting next to Gandhi at the AICC General Session, 1942</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>World War II broke out in 1939 when <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany">Nazi Germany</a> invaded <a href="/wiki/Poland">Poland</a>. Initially, Gandhi favoured offering "non-violent moral support" to the British effort, but other Congressional leaders were offended by the unilateral inclusion of India in the war, without consultation of the people's representatives. All Congressmen resigned from office.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> After long deliberations, Gandhi declared that India could not be party to a war ostensibly being fought for democratic freedom, while that freedom was denied to India itself. As the war progressed, Gandhi intensified his demand for independence, drafting a resolution calling for the British to <i><a href="/wiki/Quit_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Quit India">Quit India</a></i>. This was Gandhi's and the Congress Party's most definitive revolt aimed at securing the British exit from India.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Right_against_might.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Right_against_might.jpg/170px-Right_against_might.jpg" width="170" height="183" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Right_against_might.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
"I want world sympathy in this battle of <a href="/wiki/Might_makes_right" title="Might makes right">right against might</a>" – <a href="/wiki/Dandi,_Gujarat" title="Dandi, Gujarat">Dandi</a> 5 April 1930</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gandhi was criticised by some Congress party members and other Indian political groups, both pro-British and anti-British. Some felt that not supporting Britain more in its struggle against Nazi Germany was unethical. Others felt that Gandhi's refusal for India to participate in the war was insufficient and more direct opposition should be taken, while Britain fought against Nazism yet continued to contradict itself by refusing to grant India Independence. <i>Quit India</i> became the most forceful movement in the history of the struggle, with mass arrests and violence on an unprecedented scale.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> Thousands of freedom fighters were killed or injured by police gunfire, and hundreds of thousands were arrested. Gandhi and his supporters made it clear they would not support the war effort unless India were granted immediate independence. He even clarified that this time the movement would not be stopped if individual acts of violence were committed, saying that the <i>"ordered anarchy"</i> around him was <i>"worse than real anarchy."</i> He called on all Congressmen and Indians to maintain discipline via <a href="/wiki/Ahimsa">ahimsa</a>, and <i>Karo Ya Maro</i> ("Do or Die") in the cause of ultimate freedom.</p>
<p>Gandhi and the entire Congress Working Committee were arrested in <a href="/wiki/Bombay" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombay">Bombay</a> by the British on 9 August 1942. Gandhi was held for two years in the <a href="/wiki/Aga_Khan_Palace">Aga Khan Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pune">Pune</a>. It was here that Gandhi suffered two terrible blows in his personal life. His 50-year old secretary <a href="/wiki/Mahadev_Desai">Mahadev Desai</a> died of a heart attack 6&#160;days later and his wife Kasturba died after 18 months imprisonment on 22 February 1944; six weeks later Gandhi suffered a severe <a href="/wiki/Malaria">malaria</a> attack. He was released before the end of the war on 6 May 1944 because of his failing health and necessary surgery; the Raj did not want him to die in prison and enrage the nation. He came out of detention to an altered political scene - the <a href="/wiki/All-India_Muslim_League" title="All-India Muslim League">Muslim League</a> for example, which a few years earlier had appeared marginal, "now occupied the centre of the political stage" <sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> and the topic of Jinnah's campaign for Pakistan was a major talking point. Gandhi met Jinnah in September 1944 in Bombay but Jinnah rejected, on the grounds that it fell short of a fully independent Pakistan, his proposal of the right of Muslim provinces to opt out of substantial parts of the forthcoming political union.</p>
<p>Although the Quit India movement had moderate success in its objective, the ruthless suppression of the movement<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" title="The text in the vicinity of this tag needs clarification or removal of jargon from April 2011" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">clarification needed</a></i>]</sup> brought order to India by the end of 1943. At the end of the war, the British gave clear indications that power would be transferred to Indian hands. At this point Gandhi called off the struggle, and around 100,000 political prisoners were released, including the Congress's leadership.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Partition_of_India">Partition of India</span></h2>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India">Partition of India</a></div>
<p>While the <a href="/wiki/Indian_National_Congress">Indian National Congress</a> and Gandhi called for the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> to <a href="/wiki/Quit_India_Movement" title="Quit India Movement">quit India</a>, the <a href="/wiki/All-India_Muslim_League" title="All-India Muslim League">Muslim League</a> passed a resolution for them to divide and quit, in 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> The Mahatma is believed to have been opposed to the partition during independence and suggested an agreement which required the Congress and Muslim League to cooperate and attain independence under a provisional government, thereafter, the question of partition could be resolved by a plebiscite in the districts with a Muslim majority.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Jinnah</a> called for <a href="/wiki/Direct_Action_Day" title="Direct Action Day">Direct Action</a>, on 16 August 1946, Gandhi was infuriated and visited the most riot prone areas to stop the massacres, personally.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> He made strong efforts to unite the Indian Hindus, Muslims and Christians and struggled for the emancipation of the "<a href="/wiki/Untouchability" title="Untouchability">untouchables</a>" in Hindu society.<sup id="cite_ref-T.C3.B8nnesson_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T.C3.B8nnesson-49"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On the 14<sup>th</sup> and 15<sup>th</sup> of August, 1947 the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Independence_Act_1947" title="Indian Independence Act 1947">Indian Independence Act</a> was invoked and the following carnage witnessed a displacement of up to 12.5 million people in the former <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="British Indian Empire">British Indian Empire</a> with an estimated loss of life varying from several hundred thousand to a million.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> But for his teachings, the efforts of his followers, and his own presence, there would have been much more bloodshed during the partition, according to prominent Norwegian historian, <a href="/wiki/Jens_Arup_Seip">Jens Arup Seip</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stanley_Wolpert">Stanley Wolpert</a>'s words sum up Gandhi's role and views on the partition perfectly:</p>
<blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px; display:table;">
<p>Their plan to carve up British India was never approved of or accepted by Gandhi, however, who realised too late that his closest comrades and disciples were more interested in power than principle, and that his own vision had long been clouded by the illusion that the struggle he led for India's freedom was a nonviolent one.</p>
<p style="text-align: right;">– <cite>Stanley Wolpert, <i>Gandhi's Passion, The Life and Legacy of Mahatma Gandhi</i>, Oxford University Press</cite></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination">Assassination</span></h2>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi">Assassination of Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Memorial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Gandhi_Memorial.jpg/220px-Gandhi_Memorial.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Memorial.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Raj_Ghat_and_other_memorials" title="Raj Ghat and other memorials" class="mw-redirect">Raj Ghat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delhi">Delhi</a> is a memorial to Mahatma Gandhi that marks the spot of his cremation</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhis_ashes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gandhis_ashes.jpg/220px-Gandhis_ashes.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhis_ashes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Gandhi's ashes at Aga Khan Palace (Pune, India)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 30 January 1948, Gandhi was shot while he was walking to a platform from which he was to address a prayer meeting. The assassin, <a href="/wiki/Nathuram_Godse">Nathuram Godse</a>, was a Hindu nationalist with links to the extremist <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Mahasabha" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu Mahasabha">Hindu Mahasabha</a>, who held Gandhi responsible for weakening India by insisting upon a payment to Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Godse and his co-conspirator <a href="/wiki/Narayan_Apte">Narayan Apte</a> were later tried and convicted; they were executed on 15 November 1949. Gandhi's memorial (or <i>Samādhi</i>) at <a href="/wiki/Raj_Ghat_and_other_memorials" title="Raj Ghat and other memorials" class="mw-redirect">Rāj Ghāt</a>, New Delhi, bears the epigraph "Hē Ram", (<a href="/wiki/Devanagari">Devanagari</a>: <i>हे&#160;! राम</i> or, <i>He <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Rām</span></a></i>), which may be translated as "Oh God". These are widely believed to be Gandhi's last words after he was shot, though the veracity of this statement has been disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a> addressed the nation through radio:<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>"Friends and comrades, the light has gone out of our lives, and there is darkness everywhere, and I do not quite know what to tell you or how to say it. Our beloved leader, Bapu as we called him, the father of the nation, is no more. Perhaps I am wrong to say that; nevertheless, we will not see him again, as we have seen him for these many years, we will not run to him for advice or seek solace from him, and that is a terrible blow, not only for me, but for millions and millions in this country." – <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a>'s <a href="http://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Light_Has_Gone_Out" class="extiw" title="s:The Light Has Gone Out">address to Gandhi</a></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Gandhi's ashes were poured into urns which were sent across India for memorial services. Most were immersed at the <a href="/wiki/Sangam_at_Allahabad" class="mw-redirect" title="Sangam at Allahabad">Sangam at Allahabad</a> on 12 February 1948 but some were secretly taken away.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-2008-ashes_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2008-ashes-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1997, <a href="/wiki/Tushar_Gandhi">Tushar Gandhi</a> immersed the contents of one urn, found in a bank vault and reclaimed through the courts, at the Sangam at Allahabad.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-2008-ashes_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2008-ashes-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Some of the Mahatma's ashes were scattered at the source of the Nile River near Jinja, Uganda, and a memorial plaque marks the event. On 30 January 2008 the contents of another urn were immersed at <a href="/wiki/Girgaum_Chowpatty">Girgaum Chowpatty</a> by the family after a Dubai-based businessman had sent it to a <a href="/wiki/Mumbai">Mumbai</a> museum.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-2008-ashes_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2008-ashes-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Another urn has ended up in a palace of the <a href="/wiki/Aga_Khan">Aga Khan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pune">Pune</a><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-2008-ashes_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2008-ashes-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> (where he had been imprisoned from 1942 to 1944) and another in the <a href="/wiki/Self-Realization_Fellowship_Lake_Shrine">Self-Realization Fellowship Lake Shrine</a> in Los Angeles.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> The family is aware that these enshrined ashes could be misused for political purposes but does not want to have them removed because it would entail breaking the shrines.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-2008-ashes_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2008-ashes-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Principles.2C_practices_and_beliefs">Principles, practices and beliefs</span></h2>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gandhism">Gandhism</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Truth">Truth</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:God_is_Truth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/God_is_Truth.jpg/220px-God_is_Truth.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:God_is_Truth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
"God is truth. The way to truth lies through <a href="/wiki/Ahimsa">ahimsa</a> (non-violence)" – <a href="/wiki/Sabarmati" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabarmati">Sabarmati</a> 13 March 1927</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gandhi dedicated his life to the wider purpose of discovering <a href="/wiki/Truth">truth</a>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Satya">Satya</a></i>. He tried to achieve this by learning from his own mistakes and conducting experiments on himself. He called his autobiography <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_My_Experiments_with_Truth">The Story of My Experiments with Truth</a></i>.</p>
<p>Gandhi stated that the most important battle to fight was overcoming his own demons, fears, and insecurities. Gandhi summarised his beliefs first when he said "God is Truth". He would later change this statement to "Truth is God". Thus, <i>Satya</i> (Truth) in Gandhi's philosophy is "God".</p>
<p>Gandhi's commitment to truth has been questioned by Joseph Lelyveld, who claims that Gandhi asked journalists to quote "not the words that had actually come from his mouth but a version he ­authorized after his sometimes heavy editing of the transcripts".<sup id="cite_ref-GreatSoulReview_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreatSoulReview-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nonviolence">Nonviolence</span></h3>
<p>Although Gandhi was not the originator of the principle of non-violence, he was the first to apply it in the political field on a large scale.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> The concept of <a href="/wiki/Nonviolence">nonviolence</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Ahimsa">ahimsa</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Nonresistance">nonresistance</a> has a long history in Indian religious thought and has had many revivals in Hindu, Buddhist, Jain, Jewish and Christian contexts. Gandhi explains his philosophy and way of life in his autobiography <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_My_Experiments_with_Truth">The Story of My Experiments with Truth</a></i>. He was quoted as saying:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"When I despair, I remember that all through history the way of truth and love has always won. There have been tyrants and murderers and for a time they seem invincible, but in the end, they always fall&#160;— think of it, always."</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>"What difference does it make to the dead, the orphans, and the homeless, whether the mad destruction is wrought under the name of totalitarianism or the holy name of liberty and democracy?"</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>"An eye for an eye makes the whole world blind."</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>"There are many causes that I am prepared to die for but no causes that I am prepared to kill for."</p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_leper.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Gandhi_leper.jpg/220px-Gandhi_leper.jpg" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_leper.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Gandhi nurses Parchure Shastri, a <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit">sanskrit</a> scholar afflicted by <a href="/wiki/Leprosy">leprosy</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Sabarmati_Ashram">Sabarmati Ashram</a>, 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In applying these principles, Gandhi did not balk from taking them to their most logical extremes in envisioning a world where even government, police and armies were nonviolent. The quotations below are from the book "For Pacifists."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>The science of war leads one to dictatorship, pure and simple. The science of non-violence alone can lead one to pure democracy...Power based on love is thousand times more effective and permanent than power derived from fear of punishment....It is a blasphemy to say non-violence can be practiced only by individuals and never by nations which are composed of individuals...The nearest approach to purest anarchy would be a democracy based on non-violence...A society organised and run on the basis of complete non-violence would be the purest anarchy</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>I have conceded that even in a non-violent state a police force may be necessary...Police ranks will be composed of believers in non-violence. The people will instinctively render them every help and through mutual cooperation they will easily deal with the ever decreasing disturbances...Violent quarrels between labor and capital and strikes will be few and far between in a non-violent state because the influence of the non-violent majority will be great as to respect the principle elements in society. Similarly, there will be no room for communal disturbances....</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>A non-violent army acts unlike armed men, as well in times of peace as in times of disturbances. Theirs will be the duty of bringing warring communities together, carrying peace propaganda, engaging in activities that would bring and keep them in touch with every single person in their parish or division. Such an army should be ready to cope with any emergency, and in order to still the frenzy of mobs should risk their lives in numbers sufficient for that purpose. ...Satyagraha (truth-force) brigades can be organised in every village and every block of buildings in the cities. [If the non-violent society is attacked from without] there are two ways open to non-violence. To yield possession, but non-cooperate with the aggressor...prefer death to submission. The second way would be non-violent resistance by the people who have been trained in the non-violent way...The unexpected spectacle of endless rows upon rows of men and women simply dying rather than surrender to the will of an aggressor must ultimately melt him and his soldiery...A nation or group which has made non-violence its final policy cannot be subjected to slavery even by the atom bomb.... The level of non-violence in that nation, if that even happily comes to pass, will naturally have risen so high as to command universal respect.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In accordance with these views, in 1940, when invasion of the British Isles by Nazi Germany looked imminent, Gandhi offered the following advice to the British people (<i>Non-Violence in Peace and War</i>):<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>"I would like you to lay down the arms you have as being useless for saving you or humanity. You will invite Herr Hitler and Signor Mussolini to take what they want of the countries you call your possessions...If these gentlemen choose to occupy your homes, you will vacate them. If they do not give you free passage out, you will allow yourselves, man, woman, and child, to be slaughtered, but you will refuse to owe allegiance to them."</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In a post-war interview in 1946, he offered a view at an even further extreme:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"Hitler," Gandhi said, "killed five million Jews. It is the greatest crime of our time. But the Jews should have offered themselves to the butcher’s knife. They should have thrown themselves into the sea from cliffs... It would have aroused the world and the people of Germany... As it is they succumbed anyway in their millions."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>However, Gandhi realised that this level of nonviolence required incredible faith and courage, which he believed everyone did not possess. He therefore advised that everyone need not keep to nonviolence, especially if it were used as a cover for cowardice:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"Gandhi guarded against attracting to his <i>satyagraha</i> movement those who feared to take up arms or felt themselves incapable of resistance. 'I do believe,' he wrote, 'that where there is only a choice between cowardice and violence, I would advise violence.'"<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>"At every meeting I repeated the warning that unless they felt that in non-violence they had come into possession of a force infinitely superior to the one they had and in the use of which they were adept, they should have nothing to do with non-violence and resume the arms they possessed before. It must never be said of the <a href="/wiki/Khudai_Khidmatgar" title="Khudai Khidmatgar">Khudai Khidmatgars</a> that once so brave, they had become or been made cowards under <a href="/wiki/Khan_Abdul_Ghaffar_Khan" title="Khan Abdul Ghaffar Khan">Badshah Khan</a>'s influence. Their bravery consisted not in being good marksmen but in defying death and being ever ready to bare their breasts to the bullets."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Gandhi also came under some political fire for his criticism of those who attempted to achieve independence through more violent means. According to a report in the Frontline magazine, he did plead several times for the commutation of the death sentence of <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Singh">Bhagat Singh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rajguru" class="mw-redirect" title="Rajguru">Rajguru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sukhdev" class="mw-redirect" title="Sukhdev">Sukhdev</a> including a personal visit on 19 March 1931 and in a letter to the Viceroy on the day of their execution, pleading fervently for the commutation.<sup id="cite_ref-Frontline_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frontline-64"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> said that it was "nauseating" to see Gandhi, "a seditious Middle Temple lawyer, now posing as a fakir of a type well-known in the Middle East, striding half-naked up the steps of the Vice-regal palace. .. to parley on equal terms with the representative of the King-Emperor".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He continued this argument in a number of articles reprinted in Homer Jack's <i>The Gandhi Reader: A Sourcebook of His Life and Writings</i>. In the first, "Zionism and Anti-Semitism," written in 1938, Gandhi commented upon the 1930s persecution of the Jews in Germany within the context of <a href="/wiki/Satyagraha">Satyagraha</a>. He offered non-violence as a method of combating the difficulties Jews faced in Germany, stating,</p>
<blockquote>
<p>If I were a Jew and were born in Germany and earned my livelihood there, I would claim Germany as my home even as the tallest Gentile German might, and challenge him to shoot me or cast me in the dungeon; I would refuse to be expelled or to submit to discriminating treatment. And for doing this I should not wait for the fellow Jews to join me in civil resistance, but would have confidence that in the end the rest were bound to follow my example. If one Jew or all the Jews were to accept the prescription here offered, he or they cannot be worse off than now. And suffering voluntarily undergone will bring them an inner strength and joy...the calculated violence of Hitler may even result in a general massacre of the Jews by way of his first answer to the declaration of such hostilities. But if the Jewish mind could be prepared for voluntary suffering, even the massacre I have imagined could be turned into a day of thanksgiving and joy that Jehovah had wrought deliverance of the race even at the hands of the tyrant. For to the God-fearing, death has no terror.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Gandhi was highly criticised for these statements and responded in the article "Questions on the Jews" with "Friends have sent me two newspaper cuttings criticising my appeal to the Jews. The two critics suggest that in presenting non-violence to the Jews as a remedy against the wrong done to them, I have suggested nothing new...what I have pleaded for is renunciation of violence of the heart and consequent active exercise of the force generated by the great renunciation.<sup id="cite_ref-Homer-322_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Homer-322-67"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi's statements regarding Jews facing the impending <a href="/wiki/Holocaust" class="mw-redirect" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a> have attracted criticism from a number of commentators.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Buber">Martin Buber</a> wrote a sharply critical open letter to Gandhi on 24 February 1939. Buber asserted that the comparison between British treatment of Indian subjects and Nazi treatment of Jews was inappropriate; moreover, he noted that when Indians were the victims of persecution, Gandhi had, on occasion, supported the use of force.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi commented upon the 1930s persecution of the Jews in Germany within the context of <a href="/wiki/Satyagraha">Satyagraha</a>. In the November 1938 article on the Nazi persecution of the Jews quoted above, he offered non-violence as a solution:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The German persecution of the Jews seems to have no parallel in history. The tyrants of old never went so mad as Hitler seems to have gone. And he is doing it with religious zeal.
