For he is propounding a new religion of exclusive and militant nationalism in the name of which any inhumanity becomes an act of humanity to be rewarded here and hereafter. The crime of an obviously mad but intrepid youth is being visited upon his whole race with unbelievable ferocity. If there ever could be a justifiable war in the name of and for humanity, a war against Germany, to prevent the wanton persecution of a whole race, would be completely justified. But I do not believe in any war. A discussion of the pros and cons of such a war is therefore outside my horizon or province. But if there can be no war against Germany, even for such a crime as is being committed against the Jews, surely there can be no alliance with Germany. How can there be alliance between a nation which claims to stand for justice and democracy and one which is the declared enemy of both?"<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vegetarianism">Vegetarianism</span></h3>
<p>As a child, Gandhi experimented with meat-eating. This was due partially to his inherent curiosity as well as his rather persuasive peer and friend Sheikh Mehtab. The idea of vegetarianism is deeply ingrained in Hindu and <a href="/wiki/Jain" class="mw-redirect" title="Jain">Jain</a> traditions in India, and, in his native land of <a href="/wiki/Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, most Hindus are vegetarian and so are almost all Jains.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The Gandhi family was no exception. Before leaving for his studies in London, Gandhi made a promise to his mother, Putlibai and his uncle, Becharji Swami that he would abstain from eating meat, taking alcohol, and engaging in promiscuity. He held fast to his promise and gained more than a diet: he gained a basis for his life-long philosophies. As Gandhi grew into adulthood, he became a strict <a href="/wiki/Lacto_vegetarianism" title="Lacto vegetarianism">vegetarian</a>. He wrote the book <i>The Moral Basis of Vegetarianism</i><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> and several articles on the subject, some of which were published in the London Vegetarian Society's publication, <i>The Vegetarian</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> During this period, the young Gandhi became inspired by many great minds and was befriended by the chairman of the London Vegetarian Society, Dr. Josiah Oldfield.</p>
<p>Having also read and admired the work of Henry Stephens Salt, the young Mohandas met and often corresponded with the vegetarian campaigner. Gandhi spent much time advocating vegetarianism during and after his time in London. To Gandhi, a vegetarian diet would not only satisfy the requirements of the body, it would also serve an economic purpose as meat was, and still is, generally more expensive than grains, vegetables, and fruits. Also, many Indians of the time struggled with low income, thus vegetarianism was seen not only as a spiritual practice but also a practical one. He abstained from eating for long periods, using <a href="/wiki/Fasting">fasting</a> as a form of political protest. He refused to eat until his death or his demands were met. Gandhi noted in his autobiography that vegetarianism was the beginning of his deep commitment to <a href="/wiki/Brahmacharya">Brahmacharya</a>; without total control of the palate, his success in Bramacharya would likely falter.</p>
<p>Gandhi also experimented with <a href="/wiki/Fruitarianism">fruitarianism</a>, stating in his autobiography, "I decided to live on a pure fruit diet, and that too composed of the cheapest fruit possible ... Raw groundnuts, bananas, dates, lemons and olive oil composed our usual diet."<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> However, late in life he broke his discipline and started taking goat's milk on the advice of his doctor. This lapse of discipline bothered him to his dying day, and he wrote, "The memory of this action even now rankles my breast and fills me with remorse, and I am constantly thinking how to give up goat's milk." He never took dairy products obtained from cows because of his view initially that milk is not the natural diet of man, disgust for <a href="/wiki/Cow_blowing">cow blowing</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> and, specifically, because of a vow to his late mother.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nai_Talim.2C_Basic_Education">Nai Talim, Basic Education</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nai_Talim">Nai Talim</a></div>
<p><i>Nai Talim</i> is a spiritual principle which states that knowledge and work are not separate. Gandhi promoted an educational curriculum with the same name based on this pedagogical principle.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>It can be translated with the phrase 'Basic Education for all'.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the concept has several layers of meaning. It developed out of Gandhi's experience with the English educational system and with colonialism in general. In that system, he saw that Indian children would be alienated and 'career-based thinking' would become dominant. In addition, it embodied a series of negative outcomes: the disdain for <a href="/wiki/Manual_work" class="mw-redirect" title="Manual work">manual work</a>, the development of a new elite class, and the increasing problems of industrialization and urbanization.</p>
<p>The three pillars of Gandhi's pedagogy were its focus on the <i>life-long character</i> of education, its <i>social character</i> and its form as a <i>holistic process</i>. For Gandhi, education is 'the moral development of the person', a process that is by definition 'life-long'.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brahmacharya.2C_Sexuality">Brahmacharya, Sexuality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_at_Darwen_with_women.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Gandhi_at_Darwen_with_women.jpg/220px-Gandhi_at_Darwen_with_women.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_at_Darwen_with_women.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Gandhi with textile workers at <a href="/wiki/Darwen">Darwen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lancashire">Lancashire</a>, 26 September 1931.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Gandhi was 16 his father became very ill. Being very devoted to his parents, he attended to his father at all times during his illness. However, one night, Gandhi's uncle came to relieve Gandhi for a while. He retired to his bedroom and had sex with his wife. Shortly afterward a servant came to report that Gandhi's father had just died. Gandhi felt tremendous guilt and never could forgive himself. He came to refer to this event as "double shame." The incident had significant influence in Gandhi becoming <a href="/wiki/Celibacy" title="Celibacy">celibate</a> at the age of 36, while still married.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>This decision was deeply influenced by the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Brahmacharya">Brahmacharya</a>&#160;— spiritual and practical purity&#160;— largely associated with celibacy and <a href="/wiki/Asceticism">asceticism</a>. Gandhi saw Brahmacharya as a means of becoming close with God and as a primary foundation for self-realisation. In his autobiography he tells of his battle against lustful urges and fits of jealousy with his childhood bride, <a href="/wiki/Kasturba_Gandhi" title="Kasturba Gandhi">Kasturba</a>. He felt it his personal obligation to remain celibate so that he could learn to love, rather than lust. For Gandhi, Brahmacharya meant "control of the senses in thought, word and deed.".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Within a year of his chastity vow, Gandhi's newspaper, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Opinion">Indian Opinion</a>, wrote "It is the duty of every thoughtful Indian not to marry. In case he is helpless in regard to marriage, he should abstain from sexual intercourse with his wife". Gandhi also conducted sexual experiments in his <a href="/wiki/Ashram" title="Ashram">ashrams</a>. In these ashrams, boys and girls bathed together, slept in the same beds, but were punished for any sexual talk. Men and women were segregated. Gandhi also advised that husbands should not be alone with their wives, and, when they felt sexual desire, should take a cold bath.<sup id="cite_ref-independent_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A recent biography of Gandhi, <a href="/wiki/Great_Soul:_Mahatma_Gandhi_and_His_Struggle_With_India">Great Soul: Mahatma Gandhi and His Struggle With India</a> by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lelyveld">Joseph Lelyveld</a>, has led to claims that he was "a sexual weirdo".<sup id="cite_ref-GreatSoulReview_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreatSoulReview-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The controversy surround's Gandhi's relationship with <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Kallenbach">Hermann Kallenbach</a>, a "German-Jewish architect and bodybuilder". <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Roberts">Andrew Roberts</a> for <a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a> says that Gandhi left his wife for Kallenbach in 1908. The pair continued to send letters after they were separated by Gandhi's return to India in 1914. Lelyveld has stated that the press coverage "grossly distort[s]" the overall message of the book.<sup id="cite_ref-LelyveldResponse_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LelyveldResponse-84"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> However, this did not prevent the government of the Indian state Gujarat, Gandhi's birth state, from banning the book.<sup id="cite_ref-BookBan_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BookBan-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Towards the end of his life, it became public knowledge that Gandhi had been sharing his bed for a number of years with young women.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> He explained that he did this for bodily warmth at night and termed his actions as "nature cure". Later in his life he started experimenting with brahmacharya in order to test his self-control. His letter to <a href="/wiki/Ghanshyam_Das_Birla" title="Ghanshyam Das Birla">Birla</a> in April 1945 referring to 'women or girls who have been naked with me' indicates that several women were part of his experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-parekh210_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parekh210-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> He wrote five editorials in <i>Harijan</i> discussing the practice of brahmacharya.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As part of these experiments, he initially slept with his women associates in the same room but at a distance. Afterwards he started to lie in the same bed with his women disciples and later took to sleeping naked alongside them.<sup id="cite_ref-parekh210_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parekh210-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Gandhi active-celibacy meant perfect self-control in the presence of the opposite sex. Gandhi conducted his experiments with a number of women such as Abha, the sixteen-year-old wife of his grandnephew Kanu Gandhi.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi acknowledged "that this experiment is very dangerous indeed", but thought "that it was capable of yielding great results".<sup id="cite_ref-Tidrick.2C_Kathryn_2007_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tidrick.2C_Kathryn_2007-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> His nineteen-year-old grandniece, Manu Gandhi, too was part of his experiments. Gandhi had earlier written to her father, Jaisukhlal Gandhi, that Manu had started to share his bed so that he may "correct her sleeping posture".<sup id="cite_ref-Tidrick.2C_Kathryn_2007_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tidrick.2C_Kathryn_2007-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi saw himself as a mother to these women and would refer to Abha and Manu as "my walking sticks".</p>
<p>Gandhi called Sarladevi, a married woman with children and a devout follower, his "spiritual wife". He later said that he had come close to having sexual relations with her.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> He had told a correspondent in March, 1945 that "sleeping together came with my taking up of bramhacharya or even before that"; he said he had experimented with his wife "but that was not enough".<sup id="cite_ref-Tidrick.2C_Kathryn_2007_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tidrick.2C_Kathryn_2007-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi felt satisfied with his experiments and wrote to Manu that "I have successfully practiced the eleven vows taken by me. This is the culmination of my striving for last thirty-six years. In this yajna I got a glimpse of the ideal truth and purity for which I have been striving".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to Gandhi's letters, some of his "experiments" included <a href="/wiki/Striptease" title="Striptease">stripteases</a> and other non-contact sexual activity. Explicit material has been found in his letters, although much of it was destroyed, with one reading "Vina's sleeping with me might be called an accident. All that can be said is that she slept close to me".<sup id="cite_ref-independent_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi complained of experiencing "<a href="/wiki/Ejaculation" title="Ejaculation">involuntary discharges</a>" more frequently since his return to India. He believed, however, that <a href="/wiki/Semen">semen</a> held special power, saying "one who conserves his vital fluid acquires unfailing power".<sup id="cite_ref-independent_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi had to take criticism for his experiments by many of his followers and opponents. His stenographer, R. P. Parasuram, resigned when he saw Gandhi sleeping naked with Manu.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolpert.2C_Stanley_2001_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolpert.2C_Stanley_2001-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi insisted that he never felt aroused while he slept beside her, or with Sushila Nayar or Abha. "I am sorry" Gandhi said to Parasuram, "you are at liberty to leave me today." <a href="/wiki/Nirmal_Kumar_Bose">Nirmal Kumar Bose</a>, leading anthropologist and close associate of Gandhi, parted company with him in April, 1947 post Gandhi's tour of <a href="/wiki/Noakhali_district" title="Noakhali district" class="mw-redirect">Noakhali</a>, where some sort of altercation had taken place between Gandhi and Sushila in his bedroom at midnight that caused Gandhi to slap his forehead. Bose said, "there was no immorality on part of Gandhi. Moreover Gandhi tried to conquer the feeling of sex by consciously endeavouring to convert himself into a mother of those who were under his case, whether men or women". This maternal emphasis has also been pointed out by <a href="/wiki/Dattatreya_Balkrushna_Kalelkar" class="mw-redirect" title="Dattatreya Balkrushna Kalelkar">Dattatreya Balkrushna Kalelkar</a>, a revolutionary turned disciple of Gandhi.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simplicity">Simplicity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_and_Mountbatten_drink_tea.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Gandhi_and_Mountbatten_drink_tea.jpg/220px-Gandhi_and_Mountbatten_drink_tea.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_and_Mountbatten_drink_tea.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Gandhi having tea with <a href="/wiki/Louis_Mountbatten,_1st_Earl_Mountbatten_of_Burma" title="Louis Mountbatten, 1st Earl Mountbatten of Burma">Lord Mountbatten</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gandhi earnestly believed that a person involved in public service should lead a <a href="/wiki/Simple_living" title="Simple living">simple life</a>. He first displayed this principle when he gave up wearing western-style clothing, which he associated with wealth and success. When he returned to India he renounced the western lifestyle he led in South Africa, where he had enjoyed a successful legal practice.</p>
<p>Gandhi dressed to be accepted by the poorest person in India, advocating the use of homespun cloth (<i>khadi</i>). He and his followers adopted the practice of weaving their own clothes from thread they themselves spun on a <a href="/wiki/Charkha" class="mw-redirect" title="Charkha">charkha</a>, and encouraged others to do so. While Indian workers were often idle due to unemployment, they had often bought their clothing from industrial manufacturers owned by British interests. The <a href="/wiki/Swadeshi_movement">Swadeshi movement</a> held that if Indians made their own clothes, it would deal an economic blow to the British establishment in India. Gandhian simplicity was a sign and expression of <i>swadeshi</i> principles. Consequently, the <i>charkha</i> was later incorporated into the flag of the Indian National Congress. He subsequently wore a <a href="/wiki/Dhoti">dhoti</a> for the rest of his life to express the <a href="/wiki/Simplicity">simplicity</a> of his life.</p>
<p>The practice of giving up unnecessary expenditure, embracing a simple lifestyle and washing his own clothes, Gandhi called "reducing himself to zero".<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> On one occasion he returned the gifts bestowed to him from the Natals for his diligent service to the community.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi spent one day of each week in silence. He believed that abstaining from speaking brought him <a href="/wiki/Inner_peace">inner peace</a> and made him a better listener. This influence was drawn from the Hindu principles of <i>mauna</i> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>:<span lang="sa" xml:lang="sa">मौनं</span>&#160;— silence) and <i>shanti</i> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>:<span lang="sa" xml:lang="sa">शांति</span>&#160;— peace). On such days he communicated with others by writing on paper. For three and a half years, from the age of 37, Gandhi refused to read newspapers, claiming that the tumultuous state of world affairs caused him more confusion than his own inner unrest.</p>
<p>After reading <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin">John Ruskin</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Unto_This_Last">Unto This Last</a></i>, he decided to change his lifestyle and create a commune called <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenix_Settlement&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Phoenix Settlement (page does not exist)">Phoenix Settlement</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faith">Faith</span></h3>
<p>Gandhi was born a Hindu and practised <a href="/wiki/Hinduism">Hinduism</a> all his life. As a common Hindu, he believed all religions to be equal, and rejected all efforts to convert him to a different faith. He was an avid theologian and read extensively about all major religions. He had the following to say about Hinduism:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div>Hinduism as I know it entirely satisfies my soul, fills my whole being...When doubts haunt me, when disappointments stare me in the face, and when I see not one ray of light on the horizon, I turn to the <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavad_Gita">Bhagavad Gita</a></i>, and find a verse to comfort me; and I immediately begin to smile in the midst of overwhelming sorrow. My life has been full of tragedies and if they have not left any visible and indelible effect on me, I owe it to the teachings of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>.</div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Smriti.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Gandhi_Smriti.jpg/220px-Gandhi_Smriti.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Smriti.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Birla_House" title="Birla House" class="mw-redirect">Gandhi Smriti</a></i> (The house Gandhi lodged in the last 4 months of his life has now become a monument, New Delhi)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gandhi wrote a commentary on the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> in <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_language" title="Gujarati language">Gujarati</a>. The Gujarati manuscript was translated into English by Mahadev Desai, who provided an additional introduction and commentary. It was published with a Foreword by Gandhi in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi believed that at the core of every religion was truth and love (compassion, nonviolence and <a href="/wiki/Ethic_of_reciprocity" title="Ethic of reciprocity" class="mw-redirect">the Golden Rule</a>). He also questioned what he saw as hypocrisy, malpractices, and dogma in all religions, including his own, and he was a tireless advocate for social reform in religion. Some of his comments on various religions are:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div>Thus if I could not accept Christianity either as a perfect, or the greatest religion, neither was I then convinced of Hinduism being such. Hindu defects were pressingly visible to me. If untouchability could be a part of Hinduism, it could but be a rotten part or an excrescence. I could not understand the <i>raison d'être</i> of a multitude of sects and castes. What was the meaning of saying that the Vedas were the inspired Word of God? If they were inspired, why not also the Bible and the Koran? As Christian friends were endeavouring to convert me, so were Muslim friends. Abdullah Seth had kept on inducing me to study Islam, and of course he had always something to say regarding its beauty.</div>
<div class="templatequotecite">—<a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_My_Experiments_with_Truth" title="The Story of My Experiments with Truth">Gandhi's autobiography</a></div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div>As soon as we lose the moral basis, we cease to be religious. There is no such thing as religion over-riding morality. Man, for instance, cannot be untruthful, cruel or incontinent and claim to have God on his side.</div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div>The sayings of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad">Muhammad</a> are a treasure of wisdom, not only for Muslims but for all of mankind.</div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div>I like your Christ, I do not like your Christians. Your Christians are so unlike your Christ.</div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div>God has no religion.</div>
</blockquote>
<p>Later in his life, when he was asked whether he was a Hindu, he replied, "Yes I am. I am also a Christian, a Muslim, a Buddhist and a Jew."</p>
<p>In spite of their deep reverence to each other, Gandhi and <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a> engaged in protracted debates more than once. These debates exemplify the philosophical differences between the two most famous Indians at the time. On 15 January 1934, an earthquake hit <a href="/wiki/Bihar">Bihar</a> and caused extensive damage and loss of life. Gandhi maintained this was because of the sin committed by upper caste Hindus by not letting untouchables in their temples (Gandhi was committed to the cause of improving the fate of untouchables, referring to them as <a href="/wiki/Harijan" title="Harijan">Harijans</a>. Tagore vehemently opposed Gandhi's stance, maintaining that an earthquake can only be caused by natural forces, not moral reasons, however repugnant the practice of untouchability may be.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi took a keen interest in <a href="/wiki/Theosophy">theosophy</a>. He empathised with theosophy's message of "universal brotherhood and consequent toleration", as he put it in 1926.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Swaraj.2C_Self-Rule">Swaraj, Self-Rule</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Swaraj">Swaraj</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tagore_Gandhi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Tagore_Gandhi.jpg/220px-Tagore_Gandhi.jpg" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tagore_Gandhi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Gandhi with <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a>, 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gandhi was a self-described <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_anarchism" title="Philosophical anarchism">philosophical anarchist</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> and his vision of India meant India without an underlying government.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> He once said that "the ideally nonviolent state would be an ordered anarchy."<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> While political systems are largely hierarchical, with each layer of authority from the individual to the central government have increasing levels of authority over the layer below, Gandhi believed that society should be the exact opposite, where nothing is done without the consent of anyone, down to the individual. His idea was that true <a href="/wiki/Swaraj" title="Swaraj">self-rule</a> in a country means that every person rules his or herself and that there is no state which enforces laws upon the people.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> This would be achieved over time with nonviolent conflict mediation, as power is divested from layers of hierarchical authorities, ultimately to the individual, which would come to embody the ethic of nonviolence. Rather than a system where rights are enforced by a higher authority, people are self-governed by mutual responsibilities. On returning from South Africa, when Gandhi received a letter asking for his participation in writing a world charter for human rights, he responded saying, "in my experience, it is far more important to have a charter for human duties."<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> A free India for him meant the existence of thousands of self-sufficient small communities (an idea possibly from <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Tolstoy</a>) who rule themselves without hindering others. It did not mean merely transferring a British established administrative structure into Indian hands which he said was just <i>making Hindustan into Englistan</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chapter_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chapter-108"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> He wanted to ultimately dissolve the Congress Party after independence and establish a system of <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy">direct democracy</a> in India,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> having no faith in the British styled parliamentary system.<sup id="cite_ref-Chapter_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chapter-108"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_works">Literary works</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_India.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Young_India.png/220px-Young_India.png" width="220" height="304" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_India.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<i>Young India</i>, a journal published by Gandhi</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gandhi was a prolific writer. For decades he edited several newspapers including <i><a href="/wiki/Harijan">Harijan</a></i> in Gujarati, in <a href="/wiki/Hindi" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and in the English language; <i><a href="/wiki/Indian_Opinion">Indian Opinion</a></i> while in South Africa and, <i><a href="/wiki/Young_India">Young India</a></i>, in English, and Navajivan, a Gujarati monthly, on his return to India. Later, Navajivan was also published in Hindi.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, he wrote letters almost every day to individuals and newspapers. However, Andrew Roberts writes that 'we cannot be certain that he really made all the pronouncements attributed to him, since, according to Mr. Lelyveld, Gandhi insisted that journalists file "not the words that had actually come from his mouth but a version he ­authorized after his sometimes heavy editing of the transcripts."'.<sup id="cite_ref-GreatSoulReview_36-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreatSoulReview-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi also wrote several books including his autobiography, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_My_Experiments_with_Truth" title="The Story of My Experiments with Truth">An Autobiography of My Experiments with Truth</a> ((Gujarātī "સત્યના પ્રયોગો અથવા આત્મકથા"))</i>, of which he bought the entire first edition to make sure it was reprinted.<sup id="cite_ref-GreatSoulReview_36-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreatSoulReview-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> His other autobiographies included: <i>Satyagraha in South Africa</i> about his struggle there, <i><a href="/wiki/Hind_Swaraj_or_Indian_Home_Rule">Hind Swaraj or Indian Home Rule</a></i>, a political pamphlet, and a paraphrase in Gujarati of <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin">John Ruskin</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Unto_This_Last">Unto This Last</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Unto_this_last_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unto_this_last-111"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> This last essay can be considered his programme on economics. He also wrote extensively on vegetarianism, diet and health, religion, social reforms, etc. Gandhi usually wrote in Gujarati, though he also revised the Hindi and English translations of his books.</p>
<p>Gandhi's complete works were published by the Indian government under the name <i><a href="http://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Collected_Works_of_Mahatma_Gandhi" class="extiw" title="s:The Collected Works of Mahatma Gandhi">The Collected Works of Mahatma Gandhi</a></i> in the 1960s. The writings comprise about 50,000 pages published in about a hundred volumes. In 2000, a revised edition of the complete works sparked a controversy, as Gandhian followers argue that the government incorporated the changes for political purposes. The Indian government later withdrew the revised edition.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_depictions_in_popular_culture">Legacy and depictions in popular culture</span></h2>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_artistic_depictions_of_Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi">List of artistic depictions of Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi</a></div>
<p>The word <i><a href="/wiki/Mahatma" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahatma">Mahatma</a></i>, while often mistaken for Gandhi's given name in the West, is taken from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language" class="mw-redirect">Sanskrit</a> words <i>maha</i> (meaning <i>Great</i>) and <i>atma</i> (meaning <i>Soul</i>). <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a> is said to have accorded the title to Gandhi.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> In his autobiography, Gandhi nevertheless explains that he never valued the title, and was often pained by it.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Followers_and_international_influence">Followers and international influence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USSR_stamp_M.Gandi_1969_6k.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/USSR_stamp_M.Gandi_1969_6k.jpg/220px-USSR_stamp_M.Gandi_1969_6k.jpg" width="220" height="308" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USSR_stamp_M.Gandi_1969_6k.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Mahatma Gandhi on a 1969 postage stamp of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union">Soviet Union</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahatma_Gandhi_District_in_Houston_Texas.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Mahatma_Gandhi_District_in_Houston_Texas.JPG/220px-Mahatma_Gandhi_District_in_Houston_Texas.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahatma_Gandhi_District_in_Houston_Texas.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahatma Gandhi District">Mahatma Gandhi District</a> in <a href="/wiki/Houston">Houston</a>, Texas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gandhi influenced important leaders and political movements. Leaders of the <a href="/wiki/African-American_Civil_Rights_Movement_(1955%E2%80%931968)" title="African-American Civil Rights Movement (1955–1968)">civil rights movement</a> in the United States, including <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King" class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther King">Martin Luther King</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Lawson">James Lawson</a>, drew from the writings of Gandhi in the development of their own theories about non-violence.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Anti-<a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa_in_the_Apartheid_Era" title="History of South Africa in the Apartheid Era" class="mw-redirect">apartheid</a> activist and former President of South Africa, <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>, was inspired by Gandhi.<sup id="cite_ref-Mandela-2000_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandela-2000-118"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Others include <a href="/wiki/Khan_Abdul_Ghaffar_Khan">Khan Abdul Ghaffar Khan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Steve_Biko">Steve Biko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Benigno_Aquino,_Jr.">Benigno Aquino, Jr.</a> (the Philippine opposition leader during the <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship">dictatorship</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Marcos">Ferdinand Marcos</a> and father of current Philippine president <a href="/wiki/Benigno_Aquino_III">Benigno Aquino III</a>).</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"Christ gave us the goals and Mahatma Gandhi the tactics." – <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr" class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther King Jr">Martin Luther King Jr</a>, 1955<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In his early years, the former <a href="/wiki/President_of_South_Africa">President of South Africa</a> <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> was a follower of the non-violent resistance philosophy of Gandhi.<sup id="cite_ref-Mandela-2000_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandela-2000-118"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Bhana and Vahed commented on these events as "Gandhi inspired succeeding generations of South African activists seeking to end White rule. This legacy connects him to <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>...in a sense Mandela completed what Gandhi started."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi's life and teachings inspired many who specifically referred to Gandhi as their mentor or who dedicated their lives to spreading Gandhi's ideas. In Europe, <a href="/wiki/Romain_Rolland">Romain Rolland</a> was the first to discuss Gandhi in his 1924 book <i>Mahatma Gandhi,</i> and Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Anarchist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarchist">anarchist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feminist" class="mw-redirect" title="Feminist">feminist</a> <a href="/wiki/Maria_Lacerda_de_Moura">Maria Lacerda de Moura</a> wrote about Gandhi in her work on pacifism. In 1931, notable European physicist <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> exchanged written letters with Gandhi, and called him "a role model for the generations to come" in a later writing about him.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lanza_del_Vasto">Lanza del Vasto</a> went to India in 1936 intending to live with Gandhi; he later returned to Europe to spread Gandhi's philosophy and founded the <a href="/wiki/Community_of_the_Ark">Community of the Ark</a> in 1948 (modelled after Gandhi's ashrams). <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_Slade" class="mw-redirect" title="Madeleine Slade">Madeleine Slade</a> (known as "Mirabehn") was the daughter of a British admiral who spent much of her adult life in India as a devotee of Gandhi.</p>
<p>In addition, the British musician <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon">John Lennon</a> referred to Gandhi when discussing his views on non-violence.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Cannes_Lions_International_Advertising_Festival">Cannes Lions International Advertising Festival</a> in 2007, former U.S. Vice-President and environmentalist <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore">Al Gore</a> spoke of Gandhi's influence on him.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>President of the United States <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama">Barack Obama</a> in an address to a Joint Session of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_India">Parliament of India</a> said that:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"I am mindful that I might not be standing before you today, as President of the United States, had it not been for Gandhi and the message he shared with <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">America</a> and the world." – <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama">Barack Obama</a> in an address to a Joint Session of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_India">Parliament of India</a>, 2010<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Obama at the Wakefield High School speech in Sept 2009, said that his biggest inspiration came from Mahatma Gandhi. His reply was in response to the question 'Who was the one person, dead or live, that you would choose to dine with?'. He continued that "He's somebody I find a lot of inspiration in. He inspired Dr. King with his message of nonviolence. He ended up doing so much and changed the world just by the power of his ethics."</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahatma Gandhi District">Mahatma Gandhi District</a> in <a href="/wiki/Houston">Houston</a>, Texas, United States, an ethnic Indian enclave, is named after Gandhi. The district officially received its named on 16 January 2010 when the City of Houston held a naming ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-MorenoCelebrates_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MorenoCelebrates-127"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_holidays">Global holidays</span></h3>
<p>On 15 June 2007, it was announced that the "<a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a>" has "unanimously adopted" a resolution declaring 2 October as "the <a href="/wiki/International_Day_of_Non-Violence">International Day of Non-Violence</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> 30 January of every year is observed the <a href="/wiki/School_Day_of_Non-violence_and_Peace">School Day of Non-violence and Peace</a> in schools of many countries.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> In countries with a Southern Hemisphere school calendar, it can be observed on 30 March.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">Awards</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandi_bista_Novi_Beograd.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Gandi_bista_Novi_Beograd.JPG/200px-Gandi_bista_Novi_Beograd.JPG" width="200" height="267" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandi_bista_Novi_Beograd.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Monument to Mahatma Gandhi in <a href="/wiki/New_Belgrade" class="mw-redirect" title="New Belgrade">New Belgrade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia">Serbia</a>. On the monument is written "Non-violence is the essence of all religions".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Time</i> magazine named Gandhi the <a href="/wiki/Person_of_the_Year" title="Person of the Year">Man of the Year</a> in 1930. Gandhi was also the runner-up to <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> as "<a href="/wiki/Person_of_the_Century" class="mw-redirect" title="Person of the Century">Person of the Century</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> at the end of 1999. Einstein said of Gandhi:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Mahatma Gandhi's life achievement stands unique in political history. He has invented a completely new and humane means for the liberation war of an oppressed country, and practised it with greatest energy and devotion. The moral influence he had on the conciously thinking human being of the entire civilized world will probably be much more lasting than it seems in our time with its overestimation of brutal violent forces. Because lasting will only be the work of such statesmen who wake up and strengthen the moral power of their people through their example and educational works. We may all be happy and grateful that destiny gifted us with such an enlightened contemporary, a role model for the generations to come.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><i>Time Magazine</i> named <a href="/wiki/14th_Dalai_Lama" title="14th Dalai Lama">The 14th Dalai Lama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lech_Wa%C5%82%C4%99sa">Lech Wałęsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King" class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther King">Martin Luther King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cesar_Chavez" class="mw-redirect" title="Cesar Chavez">Cesar Chavez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benigno_Aquino,_Jr.">Benigno Aquino, Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Tutu">Desmond Tutu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> as <i>Children of Gandhi</i> and his spiritual heirs to non-violence.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India">Government of India</a> awards the annual <a href="/wiki/Gandhi_Peace_Prize">Gandhi Peace Prize</a> to distinguished social workers, world leaders and citizens. <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>, the leader of South Africa's struggle to eradicate racial discrimination and segregation, is a prominent non-Indian recipient. In 2011, <i>Time</i> magazine named Gandhi as one of the top 25 political icons of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi never received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>, although he was nominated five times between 1937 and 1948, including the first-ever nomination by the <a href="/wiki/American_Friends_Service_Committee">American Friends Service Committee</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> though he made the short list only twice, in 1937 and 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-Tonnesson_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tonnesson-135"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Decades later, the Nobel Committee publicly declared its regret for the omission, and admitted to deeply divided nationalistic opinion denying the award.<sup id="cite_ref-T.C3.B8nnesson_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T.C3.B8nnesson-49"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Gandhi was nominated in 1948 but was assassinated before nominations closed. That year, the committee chose not to award the peace prize stating that "there was no suitable living candidate" and later research shows that the possibility of awarding the prize posthumously to Gandhi was discussed and that the reference to no suitable living candidate was to Gandhi.<sup id="cite_ref-Tonnesson_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tonnesson-135"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/14th_Dalai_Lama">14th Dalai Lama</a> was awarded the Prize in 1989, the chairman of the committee said that this was "in part a tribute to the memory of Mahatma Gandhi."<sup id="cite_ref-Tonnesson_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tonnesson-135"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_literature">Film and literature</span></h3>
<p>Mahatma Gandhi has been portrayed in film, literature, and in the theatre. <a href="/wiki/Ben_Kingsley">Ben Kingsley</a> portrayed Gandhi in the 1982 film <i><a href="/wiki/Gandhi_(film)" title="Gandhi (film)">Gandhi</a></i>, which won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award">Academy Award</a> for Best Picture. Gandhi is also a central theme in the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Bollywood">Bollywood</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Lage_Raho_Munna_Bhai">Lage Raho Munna Bhai</a>.</i> The 2007 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Gandhi,_My_Father">Gandhi, My Father</a></i> explores the relationship between Gandhi and his son Harilal. The 1996 film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Making_of_the_Mahatma">The Making of the Mahatma</a></i>, documents Gandhi's time in South Africa.</p>
<p>Several biographers have undertaken the task of describing Gandhi's life. Among them are: D. G. Tendulkar with his <i>Mahatma. Life of Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi</i> in eight volumes, and <a href="/wiki/Pyarelal" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyarelal">Pyarelal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sushila_Nayyar" title="Sushila Nayyar">Sushila Nayar</a> with their <i>Mahatma Gandhi</i> in 10 volumes. There is also another documentary, titled <a href="/wiki/Mahatma:_Life_of_Gandhi,_1869-1948" title="Mahatma: Life of Gandhi, 1869-1948" class="mw-redirect">Mahatma: Life of Gandhi</a>, 1869–1948, which is 14 chapters and 6 hours long.</p>
<p>In April 2010, <a href="/wiki/Great_Soul:_Mahatma_Gandhi_and_His_Struggle_With_India">Great Soul: Mahatma Gandhi and His Struggle With India</a> by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lelyveld">Joseph Lelyveld</a> was released. It contains controversial claims that Gandhi was "a sexual weirdo, a political incompetent and a fanatical faddist—one who was often downright cruel to those around him."<sup id="cite_ref-GreatSoulReview_36-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreatSoulReview-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Lelyveld has stated that the press coverage "grossly distort[s]" the overall message of the book.<sup id="cite_ref-LelyveldResponse_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LelyveldResponse-84"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> However, this did not prevent the government of the Indian state Gujarat, Gandhi's birth state, from banning the book.<sup id="cite_ref-BookBan_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BookBan-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_impact_within_India">Current impact within India</span></h3>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div>"Gandhi remains India’s patriarch, the founding father whose face is printed on the currency, but modern India is hardly a Gandhian nation, if it ever was one. His vision of a village-dominated economy was shunted aside during his lifetime as rural romanticism, and his call for a national ethos of personal austerity and nonviolence has proved antithetical to the goals of an aspiring economic and military power ... Gandhi is still revered here, and credited with shaping India’s political identity as a tolerant, secular democracy. But he can sometimes seem to hover over modern India like a parent whose expectations are rarely met."</div>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times">The New York Times</a></i>, November 2010 <sup id="cite_ref-NYT10_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT10-136"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Gandhi's birthday, 2 October, is a <a href="/wiki/National_holiday_in_India" class="mw-redirect" title="National holiday in India">national holiday in India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gandhi_Jayanti">Gandhi Jayanti</a>. India observes 30 January the day of his assassination, as <a href="/wiki/Martyrs%27_Day_(India)" title="Martyrs' Day (India)">Martyr's Day</a>, to commemorate those who gave up their lives in service of the Indian nation. There are two temples in India dedicated to Gandhi.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> One is located at <a href="/wiki/Sambalpur">Sambalpur</a> in <a href="/wiki/Odisha" class="mw-redirect" title="Odisha">Odisha</a> and the other at Nidaghatta village near Kadur in <a href="/wiki/Chikmagalur">Chikmagalur</a> district of <a href="/wiki/Karnataka">Karnataka</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="column-width: 30em; -moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-0"><b><a href="#cite_ref-0">^</a></b> Gandhi, "The Momentous Issue", <i>Young India</i>, 10 November 1921, in <i>Collected Works of Mahatma Gandhi</i>, electronic edition, <a href="http://www.gandhiserve.org/cwmg/VOL025.PDF" class="external text" rel="nofollow">vol. 25</a>, pp. 76-8; and letter to P. Kodanda Rao, 10 September 1935, in <i>Collected Works of Mahatma Gandhi</i>, electronic edition, <a href="http://www.gandhiserve.org/cwmg/VOL067.PDF" class="external text" rel="nofollow">vol. 67</a>, p. 400, where he stated: "... I found that even civil disobedience failed to convey the full meaning of the struggle. I therefore adopted the phrase civil resistance."</li>
<li id="cite_note-1"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b> <span class="citation Journal" id="CITEREFBhattacharya1997">Bhattacharya, Sabyasachi (1997). <i>The Mahatma and the Poet</i>. New Delhi: National Book Trust, India. p. 1.</span><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Mahatma+and+the+Poet&amp;rft.aulast=Bhattacharya&amp;rft.aufirst=Sabyasachi&amp;rft.au=Bhattacharya%2C%26%2332%3BSabyasachi&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.pages=p.+1&amp;rft.place=New+Delhi&amp;rft.pub=National+Book+Trust%2C+India&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/en.wikipedia.org:Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi"><span style="display: none;">&#160;</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b> Gandhi means "grocer" in Gujarati (<i>L. R. Gala, Popular Combined Dictionary, English-English-Gujarati &amp; Gujarati-Gujarati-English, Navneet</i>), or "perfumer" in Hindi (<i>Bhargava's Standard Illustrated Dictionary Hindi-English</i>).</li>
